  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_8845cd3c-6017-40ef-8c5a-09849f020822'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer21160237" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13727845389922460136">دایی جان ناپلئون = Da'i-i jan Napuli'un = My Uncle Napoleon, Iraj Pezeshkzad<br/><br/>My Uncle Napoleon (Dear Uncle Napoleon) is a coming of age novel by Iranian author Iraj Pezeshkzad published in Tehran in Persian in 1973. <br/><br/>The novel was adapted to a highly successful TV series in 1976 directed by Nasser Taghvai. The novel has been translated by Dick Davis into English.<br/><br/>The story takes place at the time of Iran's occupation by the Allied Forces during World War II. Most of the plot occurs in the narrator'</span>
  <span id="freeText13727845389922460136" style="display:none">دایی جان ناپلئون = Da'i-i jan Napuli'un = My Uncle Napoleon, Iraj Pezeshkzad<br /><br />My Uncle Napoleon (Dear Uncle Napoleon) is a coming of age novel by Iranian author Iraj Pezeshkzad published in Tehran in Persian in 1973. <br /><br />The novel was adapted to a highly successful TV series in 1976 directed by Nasser Taghvai. The novel has been translated by Dick Davis into English.<br /><br />The story takes place at the time of Iran's occupation by the Allied Forces during World War II. Most of the plot occurs in the narrator's home, a huge early 20th-century-style Iranian mansion in which three wealthy families live under the tyranny of a paranoid patriarch Uncle. <br /><br />The Uncle—who in reality is a retired low-level officer from the Persian Cossack Brigade under Colonel Vladimir Liakhov's command—claims, and in latter stages of the story actually believes that he and his butler Mash Qasem were involved in wars against the British Empire and their lackeys such as Khodadad Khan, as well as battles supporting the Iranian Constitutional Revolution; and that with the occupation of Iran by the Allied Forces, the English are now on course to take revenge on him. <br /><br />The story's narrator (nameless in the novel but called Saeed in the TV series) is a high school student in love with his cousin Layli who is Dear Uncle's daughter. <br /><br />The story revolves around the narrator's struggles to stall Layli's pre-arranged marriage to her cousin Puri, while the narrator's father and Dear Uncle plot various mischiefs against each other to settle past family feuds. <br /><br />A multitude of supporting characters, including police investigators, government officials, housewives, a medical doctor, a butcher, a sycophantic preacher, servants, a shoeshine man, and an Indian or two provide various entertaining sequences throughout the development of the story.<br /><br />‏‫‭‭‭My uncle Napoleon‏‫‭‭‭/ ‎a novel by Iraj Pezeshkzad‬‏‫‭‭‭; translated from the Persian by Dick Davis. Washington D.C‬.‏‫‭‭‭: ‎Mage Publishers‬‏‫‬‭, 1996, In 507 Pages.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: ماه اکتبر سال 1975میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: دائی جان ناپلئون؛ نویسنده: ابرج پزشکزاد؛ تهران، صفیعلیشاه، 1351؛ در 459ص؛ چاپ دهم 1356؛ چاپ دوازدهم 1382 در 540ص؛ چاپ دیگر تهران، فرهنگ معاصر، 1395، در 714ص؛ شابک 9786001051197؛ موضوع داستانهای نویسندگان ایرانی - سده 20م<br /><br />کتاب را در همان سالهای نخست انتشار خواندم، با دوستانی که خوانده بودند، جمله ها را در خیابانها واگویی میکردیم و میخندیدیم؛ کتاب بیمانند و ماندگاری است؛ جناب پزشکزاد دست مریزاد؛<br /><br />نقل از کتاب: والله، بابام جان...؛ دروغ چرا؟...؛ تا قبر آآآآ...؛ اونکه ما دیدیم اینجوری ست که وقتی خاطر یکی را میخواهی...؛ آنوقتی که نمیبینیش، توی دلت پنداری یخ میبنده...؛ وقتی میبینیش، یک هورمی توی این دلت بلند میشه، پنداری تنور نانوایی روشن کرده اند...؛ همه چیز دنیا را، همه ی مال و منال دنیا را، برای اون میخواهی، پنداری حاتم طائی شده ای...؛ خلاصه آرام نمیگیری مگر اینکه آن دختر را برایت شیرینی بخورند...؛ اما این هم هست که خدای نکرده اون دختر را به یکی دیگر شوهرش بدهند، آنوقت دیگه واویلا...؛ ما یک همشهری داشتیم، خاطرخواه شده بود...؛ یک شب آن دختره را برای یکی دیگه شیرینی خوردند، صبح آن همشهری ما زد به بیابان، تا حالا که بیست سال گذشته، هنوز هیچکس نفهمیده چی شد، ...؛ پنداری دود شد رفت آسمان ...؛ پایان نقل از صفحه ی چهارده کتاب؛ این کتاب، به انگلیسی نیز ترجمه شده است<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 21/05/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>
  <a data-text-id="13727845389922460136" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1458389996" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1458389996" />
    <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten"><img alt="Jeffrey Keeten" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1301087352p2/3427339.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1458389996?book_show_action=true">Dec 02, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeffrey Keeten" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten">Jeffrey Keeten</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780934211482" href="/book/show/1594517.My_Uncle_Napoleon">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jeffrey by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/24403901-e">E</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3427339-jeffrey-keeten?shelf=iranian">iranian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1458389996" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3348021491403788661"><b>”Once upon a time I was like you, too...very sensitive...very melancholy...but time changed me...a person’s body is formed in the workshop of his mother’s body, but a person’s soul in the workshop of the world.”</b><br/><br/>Our unnamed narrator is a teenage boy who finds himself pulled in all directions by his own burgeoning feelings of love, the squabbles of his extended family, and the generally confusing advice he gets from everyone he knows. He knows one thing for sure, that he loves his cousin Layli, an</span>
  <span id="freeText3348021491403788661" style="display:none"><b>”Once upon a time I was like you, too...very sensitive...very melancholy...but time changed me...a person’s body is formed in the workshop of his mother’s body, but a person’s soul in the workshop of the world.”</b><br /><br />Our unnamed narrator is a teenage boy who finds himself pulled in all directions by his own burgeoning feelings of love, the squabbles of his extended family, and the generally confusing advice he gets from everyone he knows. He knows one thing for sure, that he loves his cousin Layli, and if he can’t have her for his own, he will just have to kill himself. <br /><br />The drama of young love, so easy to believe that it is all consuming. <br /><br />His main obstacle turns out to be his own father, not for the traditional reason that his father doesn’t feel the girl is a good match or the family is not good enough, but because his father has embroiled himself in a personal vendetta to take Layli’s father down a peg or two. <br /><br />Revenge, as they say, is best served cold, but our narrator’s father is too clever, too ambitious, too impulsive to let the slights against his character remain unchallenged. The Dear Uncle Napoleon is the patriarch of the family. None of his brothers and sisters have moved very far away; in fact, they all live around the same garden and courtyard. Anything that happens to any of them is quickly known by all. <br /><br />They are all obsessed with one another’s business.<br /><br />Dear Uncle Napoleon fought in the war, and his stories and his memories about his exploits have grown from minnows to whales. It isn’t completely his fault; his manservant, Mash Qasem, over the years has inserted himself into his master’s memories of the war and continues to add his own fabrications to the odes of war. <br /><br />Dear Uncle Napoleon has always been an admirer of Napoleon. Fortunately, his delusions about himself have not expanded to the point that he believes that he actually is Napoleon, although as the plot unfolds it becomes touch and go as to whether his mind will remain tethered by slender strands to the truth or whether he will completely be taken over by his own delusions. Not helping the situation is that the British have invaded Iran (the book is set during WWII), and he is convinced that they will arrest and execute him for his daring feats against them in the past war. <br /><br />The narrator’s father has a fine time playing on those fears. <br /><br />There is another cousin, Puri, a horse faced young man scared of guns and women in equal measure, whom Dear Uncle has promised will be married to the beautiful Layli. <br /><br /><b>OH NO!!!</b><br /><br />Uncle Asadollah Mirza, who happens to be my favorite character in the book, is a philandering admirer of widows and wives. He is one of many who have known the charms of Tahereh. <i>”I became aware of a twinkle in Asadollah Mirza’s eye. When I looked in the direction he was looking I saw in the dimly lit porchway the beautiful glittering eyes of Tahereh.”</i> She is not only beautiful but frequently available. A man can easily find himself in her arms despite the fact that her husband, Shir Ali the Butcher, has killed two men by cleaving them in two and beat up several others who dared to try and woo his wife. <i>”Shir Ali, the local butcher, was a horrifying man. He was well over six feet tall; his whole body, from head to toe, was covered in tattoos, and there were numerous knife scars visible on his head. His character and temperament fitted his terrifying body exactly.”</i><br /><br />Asadollah Mirza is a lover not a fighter. Lust is a sacred feeling for him and must not be ignored. Luckily for him, Shir Ali has found religion and has set aside his cleaver for a leg of mutton as his weapon of vengeance. Asadollah wouldn’t survive his neck cleaved in two, but he might survive a broken skull.<br /><br />In his mind, as well as the minds of many other men, the allure of Tahereh is worth the risk.<br /><br />So our Narrator, without other resources for advice, turns to Asadollah who tells him the same thing over and over that he must take Layli to San Francisco. Somewhere along the line the family has seen the movie <i>San Francisco</i> (1936), starring Clark Gable and Jeanette MacDonald. The starlet has become the symbol in their minds, representing sexual allurement, and taking someone to San Francisco is a euphemism for matching the stem with the flower petals (I guess describing a euphemism with another euphemism isn’t exactly a definition, but if you haven’t figured out what I’m referring to you might identify perfectly fine with our naive Narrator.). <br /><br />By making love to Layli, our hapless Narrator will force the hand of the Dear Uncle, and he will have to let them marry or face potentially embarrassing the family with a pregnancy out of wedlock. The plot, of course, must take a different turn. <br /><br />The police are frequently called to the family compound for anything from potential murder to a missing watch. The police add even more humor to a humorous novel. Their interrogation methods are basically to keep their suspects from thinking too much. <i>”Your answer? Quick, now, immediately, at the double!”</i> This leads to some hilarious answers that lead to more and more trouble for the family. <br /><br />Mash Qasem begins every statement with: <i>”Why should I lie?”</i> which makes everyone distrust what he is about to say even more. He isn’t alone; all the characters seem quite comfortable with swearing on everything holy that they are not lying when they are most assuredly telling incredible whoppers. <br /><br />Everyone is trying to manipulate everyone else. Problems are exasperated by more and more meddling. Those too clever prove too stupid. Those too stupid prove to be too clever. All the characters seem to have too much time on their hands. The plot is like trying to watch three events simultaneously at a circus. It is truly an amazing book and considered by many to be the masterpiece of Persian literature. I haven’t read enough literature from Iran to make that judgment, but I will certainly agree that it is a wonderful book full of contradictions as to how I perceived Iran. There is rampant adultery, the consuming of vast quantities of alcohol, and the breaking of many Islamic commandments. All of which contribute to several scenes that made me laugh out loud. I think Iraj Pezeshkzad was poking fun at the conservative veneer that exists over the entire nation. Certainly, it is exaggerated for comedic effect. <br /><br />The book was banned at one time in Iran. Pezeshkzad lives in France due to the fact that he was too politically active in the 1970s to live comfortably in the country of his birth. The book was made into a very popular TV series. There are few people in Iran who haven’t met Mash Qasem, Asadollah Mirza, or Dear Uncle Napoleon, either through their TV sets or coming to life in their minds through the printed pages of possibly a black market copy of the book. Iranians are discouraged to read the book, but I will say I for one encourage not only Iranians, but people of all countries to read this marvelous addition to literature. These characters will imprint themselves on you, and for most everyone they will recognize someone in their own family who could have been a member of this cast. I want to thank my friend E____ who recommended this book to me. E____, may you someday experience the same freedoms in your country that I do in mine. <br /><br />If you wish to see more of my most recent book and movie reviews, visit <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.jeffreykeeten.com">http://www.jeffreykeeten.com</a><br />I also have a Facebook blogger page at:<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten">https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="3348021491403788661" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_220256614" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/220256614" />
    <a title="Judy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3131238-judy"><img alt="Judy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1358272075p2/3131238.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/220256614?book_show_action=true">Oct 07, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Judy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Judy" href="/user/show/3131238-judy">Judy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780812974430" href="/book/show/25866.My_Uncle_Napoleon">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3131238-judy?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3131238-judy?shelf=2014-reads">2014-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3131238-judy?shelf=chunksters">chunksters</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3131238-judy?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3131238-judy?shelf=iran">iran</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer220256614" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9162089214973255337">I LOVED this book from page 1 to page 503! My interest in the story never lagged despite its length. <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25866.My_Uncle_Napoleon' title='My Uncle Napoleon by Iraj Pezeshkzad' rel='nofollow noopener'>My Uncle Napoleon</a> spins the story of a teenage boy (narrator) who falls in love with his first cousin, Layli, the daughter of "Dear Uncle Napoleon" the head of the family. The problem is that Layli is promised in marriage to her older cousin, Puri. What unfolds as the young man seeks to gain Layli's hand in marriage is utterly hilarious because of the wonderful characters.<br/><br/>Although referred to as </span>
  <span id="freeText9162089214973255337" style="display:none">I LOVED this book from page 1 to page 503! My interest in the story never lagged despite its length. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25866.My_Uncle_Napoleon" title="My Uncle Napoleon by Iraj Pezeshkzad" rel="nofollow noopener">My Uncle Napoleon</a> spins the story of a teenage boy (narrator) who falls in love with his first cousin, Layli, the daughter of "Dear Uncle Napoleon" the head of the family. The problem is that Layli is promised in marriage to her older cousin, Puri. What unfolds as the young man seeks to gain Layli's hand in marriage is utterly hilarious because of the wonderful characters.<br /><br />Although referred to as "Dear Uncle", Napoleon is anything but "dear". Crafty, lusty Uncle Asadollah Mirza advises our narrator to "go to San Francisco" (have sex) with Layli. His crackpot schemes to avoid family troubles, manipulate Dear Uncle will have the reader laughing. Uncle Napoleon's man servant, Mash Qasem, entertains with his quirk of answering questions with "Well, why should I lie? To the grave it's ah...ah..."and his added embellishments to Uncle Napoleon's war stories. If there's not enough humor between these two characters, there's always crazy Qamar, the lying, cheating Dustali Khan, the narrator's sabotaging father, a murderous butcher with a cheating wife and a vengeful, knife-wielding Aunt.<br /><br />Overall, the most entertaining book I have read in ages. Highly recommend!</span>
  <a data-text-id="9162089214973255337" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/925243385?book_show_action=true">Apr 30, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer925243385" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6846302683003227063">If you don't fancy reading my long review, read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/220256614' rel='nofollow noopener'>Judy's</a> which sums up what I liked about it very well!<br/><br/>Still with me? Ok, I'll convey my main impressions in the first two paragraphs, after that, you have another opportunity to leave<br/><br/>When I read that this book had been made into a <a href='https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=hwDq_j1hMCI<br%20/>' rel='nofollow noopener'>TV series</a>, I should have anticipated what it would be like: it is a hologram. Every part of it contains the image of the whole; the drama runs in place, the characters are inalterable (and apart from the nameless narrator </span>
  <span id="freeText6846302683003227063" style="display:none">If you don't fancy reading my long review, read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/220256614" rel="nofollow noopener">Judy's</a> which sums up what I liked about it very well!<br /><br />Still with me? Ok, I'll convey my main impressions in the first two paragraphs, after that, you have another opportunity to leave<br /><br />When I read that this book had been made into a <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=hwDq_j1hMCI&lt;br%20/&gt;" rel="nofollow noopener">TV series</a>, I should have anticipated what it would be like: it is a hologram. Every part of it contains the image of the whole; the drama runs in place, the characters are inalterable (and apart from the nameless narrator and his beloved cousin, who have no personalities at all, are a broadly 'unsympathetic' bunch), and the elements of the comedy are always the same. Fortunately, these are all entirely adequate to the task of keeping me entertained for five hundred pages.<br /><br />To be honest, I'm quite surprised I enjoyed it so much as I don't think I would have read this if I had been told it was a farce relying for most of its laughs on innuendo and bodily functions, especially of the penis. Clearly, Pezeshkzad has the genius of imbuing his comedy with sparkling life. The characteristic exaggerations and romance of Iranian speech, re-exaggerated by the author, are a treat non-Iranians can enjoy as the countless shockingly disingenuous dramatic oaths on the souls and deaths of loved ones and holy figures sometimes becomes a joke in itself.<br /><br />Had enough? OK, off you go, thanks for reading so far = )<br /><br /><b>Attempted Feminist Reading of Daei Jan Napoleon</b>*<br />There is one female character who speaks at any length: Aziz al-Saltaneh, whose terrifying volatility is early on highlighted by her attempt to cut off her cheating husband's penis. In addition, Farrokh Laqa, an unpleasant busybody permanently dressing in black and going to funerals, has a few lines, and the mother of cadet officer Ghiasabadi, whose unattractive appearance is her main attribute, speaks a little. For comparison, there are five main male characters who speak about as much as or more than Aziz al-Saltaneh, and many more minor ones who speak much more than any other woman<br /><br />Three women exist to be sexually attractive and potentially available to seduce or be seduced; they are mostly discussed in their absence. Layli, the narrator's beloved, is spared ever being physically described, but she barely speaks. The narrator's mother and young sister, and the wives of 'uncle colonel' and Daie Jan himself barely exist. I don't think there is a single scene in which two women speak to each other.<br /><br />Arguably the most complete, sympathetic female character is Qamar, the teenage daughter of Aziz al-Saltaneh, described in near-enough-author-voice as 'simple' (I do not know what this is translated from) and by unsympathetic characters as 'not all there' 'not right in the head' and 'crazy'. The latter is particularly inaccurate as Qamar seems to be very sane: honest, consistent and cheerful except when she is threatened with an abortion against her will. This is only one of the ways in which she is abused. She is, apparently, raped by a family member, and coerced (under the threat of a dangerous abortion) into marrying an older man. Uncle Asadollah, perhaps the most sympathetic character overall and thus closely identified with the author, obviously feels for her and treats her kindly. She is described as attractively 'chubby' (as are other attractive women - I suppose it's at least refreshing not to have a less unhealthy standard of sexual suitability) and her body is subjected to various narrative commodifications, but she does emerge as a person with a will. I like her a lot, and I wish we heard more from her.<br /><br />The strategy of having the story told from the perspective of a lovesick teenager, even if his endless eavesdropping is an utterly transparent device, allows a clear eyed and 'innocent' (though certainly not unbiased) perspective, a thin veil for straight author voice. <br /><br />Further distancing through other characters allows Pezeshkzad to poke fun at the (mainly) classism racism and patriarchal attitudes of others. However, to some extent these are conferred in by the author. Extreme male jealousy and references to wife-murder are satirised, and I sense a strong sense of injustice at the cruel prohibition against men displaying emotion: a crying boy being subjected to 'this one's a girl, get the barber and cut his willy off' could hardly be more appalling. On the other hand, in addition to the problems with female characters, the text raises scant objection to the abuse of Qamar. Uncle Asadollah's view that sex is the best thing in life and in particular that sex with a man solves all a woman's problems is distanced from the author by the narrator's firmly chaste and chivalrous attitude to his love object: 'I'm in love with her Uncle Asadollah; dirty thoughts like that have never entered my head'.<br /><br />To an extent, racism against Indian people is sent up - Daei Jan* is so racist he can hardly bear to speak to an Indian, while Uncle Asadollah, perhaps the most sympathetic character overall and thus closely identified with the author, shows no sign of being so, and enthusiastically befriends one whose wife takes his fancy. However, Asadollah describes an Arab in a very unpleasant stereotypical way. <br /><br />The narrator's father is a victim of Daei Jan* and his circle's extreme classism, and his revenge involves constantly inviting people of objectionable class status into Daie Jan's house and presence whenever he can. Asadollah directly criticises Daie Jan*'s self-importance as both unreasonable and disingenuous. However, he himself is of 'noble' origins and his virtue consists in wearing this status lightly and gracefully and behaving in the Iranian tradition of hospitality, conviviality, sympathy and helpfulness to all. Stereotypes of both upper and lower class people are embodied and mined for comedy.<br /><br /><b>The TV Series</b><br />I have a very little Farsi, but having read the book I found I could watch the series without subtitles, follow the action and enjoy it, and get more out of my reading. Indeed, I watched two hours of it while I was doing the ironing! This is surely testament to really brilliant acting and adaptation, and the inevitable humanisation of characters who have only a minimal embodiment in the novel. By watching, you can better appreciate Mash Qasem's funny habits of speech and the imposing presence of Daei Jan*. Watching gives a sense of place that the novel spends little time creating. The women are dressed in a relaxed style, in colourful dresses, and for most veils are only worn for religious observance.<br /><br /><b>The Translation</b><br />I'm not really qualified to comment as I can barely read Farsi. However, Dick Davis' translation is so natural and light-footed that I would have completely forgotten the book was not written in English if I had not occasionally mentally un-translated words and phrases to feel them in Farsi, which was an additional pleasure. Comedy must be very difficult to render in another language, but there is never a hint of strain. Davis' approach to the art of translation is discussed a little in <a href="http://ajammc.com/2014/07/08/rewriting-hafez-persian-poetry/&lt;br /&gt;" rel="nofollow noopener">this article</a> in relation to classic poetry. <br /><br />*I know it is very obnoxious of me to untranslate the title of the novel and the name of its main character, but Daei Jan (dear maternal uncle) sounds so much more natural than Dear Uncle, which makes me feel I'm writing a letter.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6846302683003227063" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText17875570552018623599" style="display:none">Did I enjoy this?<br /><br />well, why should I lie? To the grave it's...ah...ah...four fingers.<br /><br />Lovely story and wonderfully told. Heartbreaking in places, nerve jangling in others and more often than not hilarious. As funny as any adventure Bertie Wooster or Harry Flashman ever had, this novel achieved that rare feat of making me laugh out loud while reading.<br /><br />The unnamed narrator, who is little more than a cypher (but garners respect for using ever more ingenious excuses to be at the heart of the action) may well be involved in every scene but he is only one of a whole range of characters who are introduced and made effortlessly interesting. As in Wodehouse's work endless aunts and nutcase neighbours populate this Tehran Blandings, creating drama out of inconsequentials and leaving it to the Farsi equivalent of Jeeves - the super smooth and quick witted Asadollah - to save the day again and again. <br /><br />Attention! Silence. You! what are you doing without this book? Buy this book immediately. Silence. Stop. Where are you going? Get to your nearest bookstore now. Who told you to move? Silence.</span>

<div id="review_164911170" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/164911170" />
    <a title="Jessica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/86946-jessica"><img alt="Jessica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1374246859p2/86946.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/164911170?book_show_action=true">May 01, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessica" href="/user/show/86946-jessica">Jessica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780934211628" href="/book/show/1301479.My_Uncle_Napoleon">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer164911170" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6514319598661014669">I added <i>My Uncle Napoleon</i> to my Amazon wishlist after seeing it mentioned in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7603.Reading_Lolita_in_Tehran' title='Reading Lolita in Tehran by Azar Nafisi' rel='nofollow noopener'>Reading Lolita in Tehran</a> as one of the author's favorite Iranian novels. The book takes place in Tehran during World War II, during the adolescence of the unnamed male narrator, who has just (at the onset of the story) realized that he is madly in love with his cousin Layli. Professing this love publically would be scandalous not because the two are cousins ("A marriage between cousins is arranged in heaven," the charac</span>
  <span id="freeText6514319598661014669" style="display:none">I added <i>My Uncle Napoleon</i> to my Amazon wishlist after seeing it mentioned in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7603.Reading_Lolita_in_Tehran" title="Reading Lolita in Tehran by Azar Nafisi" rel="nofollow noopener">Reading Lolita in Tehran</a> as one of the author's favorite Iranian novels. The book takes place in Tehran during World War II, during the adolescence of the unnamed male narrator, who has just (at the onset of the story) realized that he is madly in love with his cousin Layli. Professing this love publically would be scandalous not because the two are cousins ("A marriage between cousins is arranged in heaven," the characters quote) but because Layli is promised to another, older cousin, and because the narrator's father and Layli's father are entrenched in a bitter rivalry from across the garden yard their houses share. <br /><br />The novel is a comedic soap opera, with the family members all out to get each other and planning elaborate actions and reactions behind each other's backs. Layli's father, the eldest and head member of the extended family, admires Napoleon to the point of beginning to equate himself with the man, becoming increasingly delusional about there being English spies everywhere ready to pay him back for his imagined victories over the British. The narrator's father wants to bring Dear Uncle Napoleon down off his high horse, but the narrator and a favorite uncle team up to try to undo everything the father does in order to maintain enough peace between the families that the narrator can go on seeing Layli. Everyone is always making sex jokes, using elaborate and ridiculous euphemisms since they consider it impolite to talk about the sex act or other bodily functions directly.<br /><br />The book is sweet and funny and enjoyable - excellent train reading fare, and I'd get home and keep on reading it long after the commute was over. It's nothing too substantial and doesn't really require a reading, but I had a good time with it. Apparently it was made into a TV show in the 1970s that was so popular in Iran that it's something of a cultural reference point for anyone living there at that time - I was hoping it would be available in English but it seems there's no such luck. It's a very visual sort of book that I imagine would translate to the screen exceptionally well.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6514319598661014669" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13509381425351533384">I have never laughed so much -- even with _The Idiot_! I don't know if that's enough to recommend a book. But it is a classic in Iran; a glorious story about the foibles of a very complicated family. <br/><br/>I relish literature like this, especially during times like this when entire countries are vilified by our government. I seek to understand and literature is one of the things I greatly depend upon to round out the more human, celebratory elements of a people, a civilization unknown to me. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1823120404" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1823120404" />
    <a title="Jacob Overmark" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41670556-jacob-overmark"><img alt="Jacob Overmark" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595533530p2/41670556.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1823120404?book_show_action=true">Dec 27, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jacob Overmark" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jacob Overmark" href="/user/show/41670556-jacob-overmark">Jacob Overmark</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780812974430" href="/book/show/25866.My_Uncle_Napoleon">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41670556-jacob-overmark?shelf=read-owned">read-owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41670556-jacob-overmark?shelf=contemporary-international">contemporary-international</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41670556-jacob-overmark?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1823120404" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9688193658036935713">You will find him in most families.<br/>The eccentric uncle, the uncle who have seen most of the world and lived to tell, have conquered and slain dragons, climbed the highest peaks and in his youth turned down an offer to father Marilyn Monroe´s yet unborn children.<br/><br/>However, in most families he does not posses the ultimate power of a patriarch.<br/><br/>This family is a bit different and even the story goes back to the time between WWI and WWII when family patterns and traditions played a more prominent role t</span>
  <span id="freeText9688193658036935713" style="display:none">You will find him in most families.<br />The eccentric uncle, the uncle who have seen most of the world and lived to tell, have conquered and slain dragons, climbed the highest peaks and in his youth turned down an offer to father Marilyn Monroe´s yet unborn children.<br /><br />However, in most families he does not posses the ultimate power of a patriarch.<br /><br />This family is a bit different and even the story goes back to the time between WWI and WWII when family patterns and traditions played a more prominent role the archetypes are still very recognizable anywhere in the world. <br /><br /><br />But, let me not give the plot away. We are witnessing a family feud from it´s very beginning and our young protagonist, who on a Friday the 13th discovers he is in love with his cousin is caught in the middle.<br />So many things happens in a relatively short timespan - but what we believe is a mischievous sound of a small cannon, which may have human origin (from within the testines, that is) will play a crucial part. <br /><br />Writing a loving satirical novel of this kind require a sharp eye for detail, just how much can you enlarge a figure and still have the audience on your side and how many social institutions and taboos can you touch upon and still not offend your audience.<br /><br />Thus this is much more of an entertaining look-back than a social satire.<br /><br />The events would of course never have taken place in your own house and family, but "I have heard something like that happened to The Amirs´ two roads down …"<br /><br />I thoroughly enjoyed the trip back to Teheran and I already miss "my" extended Iranian family. <br /><br />Looking back on a trip to San Francisco earlier in my life - it will never be the same.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /> <br /><br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="9688193658036935713" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15648740373444409120">After about forty years, I had the chance of rereading this favorite book of mine, this time in English translation. The translation is excellent, and the audiobook performance is great too, although too slow for me (I put the speed on 1.5 and then it was ok). <br/>I laughed just as hard, but there were elements in the novel that naturally had gone over my head when I first read it stealthily at the age of ten or eleven, and I am very glad that I read it again as an adult.   <span id="freeText15648740373444409120" style="display:none">After about forty years, I had the chance of rereading this favorite book of mine, this time in English translation. The translation is excellent, and the audiobook performance is great too, although too slow for me (I put the speed on 1.5 and then it was ok). <br />I laughed just as hard, but there were elements in the novel that naturally had gone over my head when I first read it stealthily at the age of ten or eleven, and I am very glad that I read it again as an adult. Reading My Uncle Napoleon at this stage of my life has another advantage as well: I have read many more books with which I can compare this one, and I must say Pezeshkzad’s masterpiece reminds me very strongly of Catch-22.<br />In short, apart from the undeniable fact that the likable characters are all male, I find everything about this book admirable.</span>

<div id="review_65358884" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/65358884" />
    <a title="Dinah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1946749-dinah"><img alt="Dinah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1239422205p2/1946749.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/65358884?book_show_action=true">Jul 28, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dinah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dinah" href="/user/show/1946749-dinah">Dinah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780934211482" href="/book/show/1594517.My_Uncle_Napoleon">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer65358884" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer575363161471974217">Goodreads doesn't have an option that I can find "Date I abandoned this book". Since this book was so popular in Iran, I wanted to understand what made it so popular. I just couldn't finish it; I skimmed to the end. It certainly reaffirms the notion that every family has its share of tiresome nuts.<br/><br/>There are lots of people hiding in bushes eavesdropping (one wonders how there could be so many strategically placed bushes); jokes about farts, more jokes about farts; jokes about sexual activity, and</span>
  <span id="freeText575363161471974217" style="display:none">Goodreads doesn't have an option that I can find "Date I abandoned this book". Since this book was so popular in Iran, I wanted to understand what made it so popular. I just couldn't finish it; I skimmed to the end. It certainly reaffirms the notion that every family has its share of tiresome nuts.<br /><br />There are lots of people hiding in bushes eavesdropping (one wonders how there could be so many strategically placed bushes); jokes about farts, more jokes about farts; jokes about sexual activity, and "Lorena Bobbitt" jokes. (There are probably puns too, but since I was reading a translation I wouldn't know.) <br /><br />The story centers around "Dear Uncle Napoleon", an aging self-appointed patriarch who endlessly relives his military exploits, which have been so embroidered over the years, that no one, least of all "Dear Uncle", can tell what is real and what is fabricated. At one of his dramatic re-tellings to the assembled family, someone farts. "Dear Uncle" decides it's the narrator's father, takes offense and the war is on. <br /><br />The family's "honor" must be upheld. He diverts the water from going to their house, he gets the local mullah to say in his sermon that the pharmacy they own uses alcohol to make their pharmaceuticals, etc. etc. The father has his own methods of revenge. When the family tries to put together a truce, it backfires. The plot is complicated by the fact that the narrator - a boy of 13 - has fallen head-over-heels for "Dear Uncle's" daughter. There are numerous cartoonishly-drawn stock characters and much of the humor lies in in their exaggerated depiction: the fat dim-witted niece, the shrewish wife, the pompous uncle, the lecherous uncle, the hot-head, the hen-pecked husband. It was just too much of the same type of joke for that long a book for me. <br /><br />The end surprised me however; it had a completely different, rather melancholy tone.</span>
  <a data-text-id="575363161471974217" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2953531780729252043">I loved this book. Funny, funny, funny. All of the characters are like real people you know. Although, this book is very specific to Iran and is set in pre-World War 2; you can encounter these characters anytime, anywhere. <br/><br/>My hat is off to the author for making me laugh and be irritated, making me love some of the personages and feel sad for the others.   <span id="freeText2953531780729252043" style="display:none">I loved this book. Funny, funny, funny. All of the characters are like real people you know. Although, this book is very specific to Iran and is set in pre-World War 2; you can encounter these characters anytime, anywhere. <br /><br />My hat is off to the author for making me laugh and be irritated, making me love some of the personages and feel sad for the others. And finally for making me feel nice warm sorrow at the end. <br /><br />The author very well portrays the "out-dated", "old-fashioned" and mostly idiotic way "traditional" folk deal with issues and non-issues. They mostly do it by Making a mountain out of anthill.<br />A book that was so close to my heart, since (even though Armenia is pretty modern) there are still people around me who think that "honor of the family" in other people's eyes is more important than right and wrong, love and marriage, health, life, death. Nothing matters, but what outsiders think of "you" and "your family". And no one really listens to one another. Tricks, plots, jokes; jokes, plots and tricks. <br /><br />And the author shows all this with light in his heart and a smile on his face. <br />Well done!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2953531780729252043" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer16660377773638286963">The forward of the edition I read called My Uncle Napoleon a classic Iranian romance/farce/satire. Given its 500-page length, I was worried about attempting to read a book so different from the fiction I usually choose. In the end, I was surprised by how much I enjoyed this comedy of errors about a dysfunctional family. Many of the characters -- especially Asadollah Mizra with his frequent sexual allusions to taking a "trip to San Francisco" -- really grew on me. My Uncle Napoleon was "laughing </span>
  <span id="freeText16660377773638286963" style="display:none">The forward of the edition I read called My Uncle Napoleon a classic Iranian romance/farce/satire. Given its 500-page length, I was worried about attempting to read a book so different from the fiction I usually choose. In the end, I was surprised by how much I enjoyed this comedy of errors about a dysfunctional family. Many of the characters -- especially Asadollah Mizra with his frequent sexual allusions to taking a "trip to San Francisco" -- really grew on me. My Uncle Napoleon was "laughing out loud" funny at many points. I just wish it were about 100 pages shorter. </span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780812974430" href="/book/show/25866.My_Uncle_Napoleon">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26416859-calzean?shelf=culture-iran">culture-iran</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26416859-calzean?shelf=calzean-2019-challenge">calzean-2019-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26416859-calzean?shelf=humour">humour</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2771992979" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15040243508243623402">A family in Tehran, 1940-41. The family is dominated by the eldest brother who unknowingly is nicknamed Uncle Napoleon for his love of the French General and his hatred of the English. Uncle Napoleon is a bully, coward and totally embellishes his experiences as a border guard many years ago. The family has to manage his ego, various relationship dramas and the arrival of the British in 1941.<br/>It is the method the book is told which makes this a unique and intriguing look at Iranian society. It is </span>
  <span id="freeText15040243508243623402" style="display:none">A family in Tehran, 1940-41. The family is dominated by the eldest brother who unknowingly is nicknamed Uncle Napoleon for his love of the French General and his hatred of the English. Uncle Napoleon is a bully, coward and totally embellishes his experiences as a border guard many years ago. The family has to manage his ego, various relationship dramas and the arrival of the British in 1941.<br />It is the method the book is told which makes this a unique and intriguing look at Iranian society. It is a satire, full of innuendos, outlandish characters and farcical scenes. It reminded me of a Gilbert and Sullivan production where misunderstandings and word plays make the basis of the humour.<br />I would be fascinated for what Iranians think of this book. It certainly shows Iranians to be just like the rest of us dealing with family squabbles, and being able to see the funny sides of things.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1929639509" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1929639509" />
    <a title="Natalie (CuriousReader)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6548813-natalie-curiousreader"><img alt="Natalie (CuriousReader)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1476533404p2/6548813.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1929639509?book_show_action=true">Sep 02, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Natalie (CuriousReader)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Natalie (CuriousReader)" href="/user/show/6548813-natalie-curiousreader">Natalie (CuriousReader)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780812974430" href="/book/show/25866.My_Uncle_Napoleon">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1929639509" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12785905156384526615">My Uncle Napoleon is a family saga of the greatest sort - filled with silly battering, in-fights and intrigues, drama and love all in one larger narrative of 1940s Iran. This novel is beloved by Iranian and international readers alike, and for good reason. Published right before the revolution, Peseshkzad writes more freely about sex, politics, religion, and general human interaction than might be supposed from a country officially known as the Islamic Republic of Iran. It is the juxtaposition o</span>
  <span id="freeText12785905156384526615" style="display:none">My Uncle Napoleon is a family saga of the greatest sort - filled with silly battering, in-fights and intrigues, drama and love all in one larger narrative of 1940s Iran. This novel is beloved by Iranian and international readers alike, and for good reason. Published right before the revolution, Peseshkzad writes more freely about sex, politics, religion, and general human interaction than might be supposed from a country officially known as the Islamic Republic of Iran. It is the juxtaposition of openness and freedom contrasted to the constrictions of honor, religion and political thought that is so interestingly played with in the novel without the narrative losing any of its entertainment factor.<br /><br />My Uncle Napoleon follows a string of quirky characters, with varying degrees of likability. To be fair, many of the characters are not particularly likeable - there are more flawed characters in this book than any I’ve seen in a while but for all their flaws, they are often relatable and almost always comedic. The title comes from the head figure of the family - he’s called Dear Uncle by most in the family, but behind his back he’s called “Uncle Napoleon” jokingly - because of the man’s deep-rooted love and respect for Bonaparte. There’s the protagonist of the story through which we are invited to see all of the family events in the first place; he falls in love with his cousin, Layli, who is Uncle Napoleon’s daughter. Other memorable characters are Dustali Khan - a truly stupid man in every sense of the word, or Asadollah - more of a suave, ‘worldly’, man than any other character in this novel, he is constantly talking of San Fransisco (which is his metaphor for sex). Then there's Mash Quasem, Uncle Napoleon’s servant who helps ignite and keep alive Uncle Napoleons delusions of his various war contributions and experiences of battles he never participated in. Much of this book really focuses on Uncle Napoleon’s delusions - and the other family member’s attempt at keeping this delusion alive when Uncle’s health is deteriorating. It leads to some utterly comedic and silly situations, that if it doesn’t have you laugh out loud at least will have you smiling at the stupidity, clumsiness, bad luck and sometimes pure tenderness these characters present again and again. <br /><br />The whole family circles around Uncle Napoleon, and on the other hand there’s the love story of our narrator - whose name I really don’t recall ever reading in the book - a thirteen year old boy who falls in love for the first time. His father who is not fond of Uncle Napoleon keeps stirring up trouble, getting into a dragged out disagreement with Uncle that lasts for the majority of the book. The disagreement between these two men keeps getting into our narrator’s way, like it has repercussions for all family members whenever the conflicts escalate. <br /><br />While this is not a perfect book, and I can’t say I was quite comfortable with how characters like Quamar was portrayed, on the whole it is a satire of Iran that give readers internationally some insight into Iran outside of its religion and without reading too much into it also serves as a very entertaining book filled with relatable types and unlikable characters, family drama and intrigue, and laughter. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12785905156384526615" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_35314941" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Simon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1483907-simon"><img alt="Simon" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/35314941?book_show_action=true">Oct 14, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Simon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Simon" rel="nofollow" href="/user/show/1483907-simon">Simon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780812974430" href="/book/show/25866.My_Uncle_Napoleon">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer35314941" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2048359170923801515">Finally finished My Uncle Napoleon the day after the book club meeting to review it. Sigh! <br/><br/>I must say I was a bit a taken back at all of the negative reviews of the book from my fellow book club members. It was variously described as banal, pointless, told nothing iran and the life of iranians, too long, written like a crappy soap opera and in the words of one member "hours of my life wasted!" <br/><br/>Out of the seven people there, only two of us liked it. <br/><br/>As for me, I did like it and would give it 4 st</span>
  <span id="freeText2048359170923801515" style="display:none">Finally finished My Uncle Napoleon the day after the book club meeting to review it. Sigh! <br /><br />I must say I was a bit a taken back at all of the negative reviews of the book from my fellow book club members. It was variously described as banal, pointless, told nothing iran and the life of iranians, too long, written like a crappy soap opera and in the words of one member "hours of my life wasted!" <br /><br />Out of the seven people there, only two of us liked it. <br /><br />As for me, I did like it and would give it 4 stars. <br /><br />What I liked about it <br />- It's satire of iranian family life under the despotic and deluded patriach of the family - the Uncle Napoleon - who tells outrageously exaggerated stories of the all his adventures fighting the british and various bandits in southern Iran at the turn of the last century <br />- How the family interacted all living in a adjoining houses joined by a garden - so different from our own way of family life<br />- How various disputes arose between various members of the extended family and how the various characters in the family conspire against each other and Uncle Napoleon in the disputes.<br />- The character of Mash Qassam as the Master's faithful servant and participant in Uncle Napoleon's stories of his outrageous exploits and battles that start to resemble Napoleon's most famous battles rather than the small squad size skirmishes that they actually were <br />- The character of Asadollah - confirmed charmer and ladies man - with his references to sex as trips to San Francisco (or Los Angeles from another angle) and how he plots with the narrator to keep the narrator's romantic dreams alive, thrawt the plans of the narrator's father against Uncle Napoleon and keep the peace in the family <br />- The various characters from the bazaar and the various situations they become involved in. It reminds me of the characters I met and talked to whilst walking through various bazaars in Iran. <br />- How the family come together to keep Uncle Napoleon in his fantasy world as his health deteriorates as a result of his paranoia over the plots of the british to kill him. <br /><br />All in all, a good read with many LOL moments. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2048359170923801515" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2835117264" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2835117264" />
    <a title="Afshin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6457420-afshin"><img alt="Afshin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565371222p2/6457420.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2835117264?book_show_action=true">Jun 22, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Afshin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Afshin" href="/user/show/6457420-afshin">Afshin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780812974430" href="/book/show/25866.My_Uncle_Napoleon">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2835117264" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10276186092395983585">The Persian Don Quixote<br/><br/>The dynamics between Uncle Napoleon and Mash Qasem reminded me of the dynamics between Don Quixote and Sancho Panza. Uncle Napoleon and Don Quixote are both delusional and authoritarian men of the battlefield. In the same way that Don Quixote likens himself to this and that great Spanish Knight, Uncle Napoleon likens his own life trajectory to that of Napoleon Bonaparte and does not shy away from making comparisons. Mash Qasem and Sancho Panza vary between being overly rat</span>
  <span id="freeText10276186092395983585" style="display:none">The Persian Don Quixote<br /><br />The dynamics between Uncle Napoleon and Mash Qasem reminded me of the dynamics between Don Quixote and Sancho Panza. Uncle Napoleon and Don Quixote are both delusional and authoritarian men of the battlefield. In the same way that Don Quixote likens himself to this and that great Spanish Knight, Uncle Napoleon likens his own life trajectory to that of Napoleon Bonaparte and does not shy away from making comparisons. Mash Qasem and Sancho Panza vary between being overly rational or exaggerating their masters’ - oftentimes military - achievements. <br /><br />The book describes the Persian mosaic elegantly and with humour. There are numerous portrayals of how inter-personal, intra-personal and social and cultural issues develop and are being dealt with in Iranian culture and arguably in many other Middle Eastern cultures e.g. feuds between family members, rumours, sexual liberation or rather the lack thereof, marriage, family reputation, etc. Reading the book, I thought about my own childhood and how my father would pick up the remote control and change the channel instantly when a “San Francisco”-scene would play out on the TV.<br /><br />One of the main themes of the book is the relationship between the freedom of the individual vis-a-vis the authority of the family. The protagonist falls in love with Layli but her family is in favour of marrying her off to Shapur or Puri without neither the protagonist nor Layli having much power over the decision.<br /><br />Furthermore there are many references to Iran’s political history: the relations with the British Empire in particular. If you have some insight in these issues - “why should I lie, to death it's ah ah ah ah” - the book will be even more of a pleasure to read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10276186092395983585" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_489990528" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/489990528" />
    <a title="Laurent" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15657919-laurent"><img alt="Laurent" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/489990528?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laurent" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laurent" href="/user/show/15657919-laurent">Laurent</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" " href="/book/show/16052545-my-uncle-napoleon">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer489990528" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7843690054488473138">As a rule, I'd say that I don't read enough Persian literature, and given that they've been producing it for several thousand years now, that's pretty inexcusable. "My Uncle Napoleon" is something of a cult novel in Iran, and I believe that the most popular TV series in Iran ever was based on it. Most importantly for Westerners like us, it gives us an insight into why Iranian politicans are always somewhat unfeasibly blaming everything on British and American intervention. The former is a partic</span>
  <span id="freeText7843690054488473138" style="display:none">As a rule, I'd say that I don't read enough Persian literature, and given that they've been producing it for several thousand years now, that's pretty inexcusable. "My Uncle Napoleon" is something of a cult novel in Iran, and I believe that the most popular TV series in Iran ever was based on it. Most importantly for Westerners like us, it gives us an insight into why Iranian politicans are always somewhat unfeasibly blaming everything on British and American intervention. The former is a particular mystery these days as I very much doubt the UK could orchestrate so much as a bus delay in Tehran. <br /><br />Essentially, this is a satirical novel about a middle-class Iranian family in the 1940s. The uncle, nicknamed Napoleon because of authoritarianism and delusions of grandeur, is convinced that the British bear him a long-term grudge because of his mostly-imagined role in the much earlier constitutional uprising (yes: Iran has on several occasions tried to become a constitutional, modern state and we've stopped it every time for our own gain). Because of this, he blames every single unfortunate event that befalls him on the British.<br /><br />With a rich cast of characters, this is an honestly hilarious novel. I don't know if it's lost much in translation from Farsi, but assuming it has, it must be absolutely fantastic in the original. So why not try and understand an unfairly caricatured nation whilst simultaneously laughing out loud? Even Ahmadinejad likes it, although he's probably only seen the TV series. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7843690054488473138" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780812974430" href="/book/show/25866.My_Uncle_Napoleon">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2497199-sandra?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2497199-sandra?shelf=five-stars">five-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2497199-sandra?shelf=fiction-literary">fiction-literary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2497199-sandra?shelf=fiction-lit-in-translation">fiction-lit-in-translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2497199-sandra?shelf=funny">funny</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1420894983" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16552150936094532056">Truly enjoyable satirical/farcical Iranian novel reminiscent of, perhaps, Vanity Fair or Jane Austen novels. It's a comedy of manners and mores, and although I'm not usually nuts about satire (I don't want amusing lectures lampooning people or cultures) this novel is a thoroughly entertaining story of family dramas and squabbles in its own right. <br/><br/>I had no idea such an Iranian novel existed, and I think it should be added to the reading lists of all lovers of literature. It's a great specimen of </span>
  <span id="freeText16552150936094532056" style="display:none">Truly enjoyable satirical/farcical Iranian novel reminiscent of, perhaps, Vanity Fair or Jane Austen novels. It's a comedy of manners and mores, and although I'm not usually nuts about satire (I don't want amusing lectures lampooning people or cultures) this novel is a thoroughly entertaining story of family dramas and squabbles in its own right. <br /><br />I had no idea such an Iranian novel existed, and I think it should be added to the reading lists of all lovers of literature. It's a great specimen of the kind of world literature that reveals both the universal aspects of human cultures and personalities as well as the particularities of each unique culture and its typical characters (and this story is full of delightful, quirky characters!), that make such works extremely enjoyable while illuminating their society to outsiders. <br /><br />Read it! It deserves a much wider audience. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16552150936094532056" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <span id="freeText2104618740901890195" style="display:none">I think for someone growing up a little removed from being Persian it was deeply amusing to see all those idiosyncrasies that went on in my family that I didn't really understand taken to the level of farce and put back into context. I wish people who wanted to know more about Iranians would read this and see people who have a deep sense of humor about themselves and making some pretty poignant social critiques via humor. <br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_1457212067" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1457212067" />
    <a title="Jonathan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7093910-jonathan"><img alt="Jonathan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1328672517p2/7093910.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1457212067?book_show_action=true">Dec 01, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonathan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonathan" href="/user/show/7093910-jonathan">Jonathan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780812974430" href="/book/show/25866.My_Uncle_Napoleon">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7093910-jonathan?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7093910-jonathan?shelf=iran">iran</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1457212067" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6551305101719798579">Like John Kennedy Toole's masterpiece "A Confederacy of Dunces" did for New Orleans, Iraj Pezeshkzad's "My Uncle Napoleon" provides unique and often hilarious insight into mid-20th century Iran. The wild collection of characters (both in the literal and figurative sense of the word) and their misadventures in Pahlavi Iran are at once entertaining and informative into the nature of Iranian society. </span>

          </span>
        </div>
